<div id="review_1486407932" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1486407932" />
    <a title="Steven" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4452312-steven"><img alt="Steven" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1449093910p2/4452312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1486407932?book_show_action=true">Dec 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven" href="/user/show/4452312-steven">Steven</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4452312-steven?shelf=hemingway">hemingway</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1486407932" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10976397849902720796">It is difficult not to relate the words of the writer-protagonist of <i>The Garden of Eden</i> to the novel itself: <blockquote><i>This was the first writing he had finished since they were married. Finishing is what you have to do, he thought. If you don't finish, nothing is worth a damn.</i> (108)</blockquote>Hemingway worked on <i>The Garden of Eden</i> for fifteen years, starting in 1946, but never finished it. After Hemingway shot himself with his favorite shotgun, his widow Mary carried the manuscript of <i>The Garden of Eden</i> in a shoppi</span>
  <span id="freeText10976397849902720796" style="display:none">It is difficult not to relate the words of the writer-protagonist of <i>The Garden of Eden</i> to the novel itself: <blockquote><i>This was the first writing he had finished since they were married. Finishing is what you have to do, he thought. If you don't finish, nothing is worth a damn.</i> (108)</blockquote>Hemingway worked on <i>The Garden of Eden</i> for fifteen years, starting in 1946, but never finished it. After Hemingway shot himself with his favorite shotgun, his widow Mary carried the manuscript of <i>The Garden of Eden</i> in a shopping bag to the publishers at Scribner. Of its 800 pages,¹ 200,000 words, and 48 chapters, they finally published the novel in 247 pages, 30 chapters, and 70,000 words. How this could happen, how it could be allowed, I haven't a clue. Either publish the man's work as he left it, or don't. Don't take the damn liberty of editing the man's work for him. And not just slightly editing it, either, by making it consistent, for instance, as the cheeky preface by Charles Scribner, Jr. suggests: they ultimately cut more than two-thirds of the novel including a long subplot. Unbelievable. I've been angry and indignant about it ever since I found out, and it makes judging the novel – at least the novel as Hemingway wrote it and intended it – nearly impossible. <br /><br />Yet all that one can do without access to the manuscript is talk about the novel as it stands, in its, one might say, butchered form. Unsurprisingly, it falls short of Hemingway's own standards; it is not nearly as polished as <i>A Farewell to Arms</i> or <i>For Whom the Bell Tolls</i>, for example. But it is, at least occasionally, Hemingway; now and then you feel his presence and voice. The story-within-a-story and the concomitant commentary on the process of writing - Hemingway's process of writing - are great. In the end, I don't know any writer who can hurt you like Hemingway can, who can make you feel just that little bit broken, broken right there with him. The happy note that marks the end of the novel rings falls, rings editorially false. <br /><br />Had Hemingway managed to finish <i>The Garden of Eden</i>, it undoubtedly would have been a better book. Perhaps it also would have been a very different book. <br /><br />¹Some scholars claim the manuscript was actually a whopping 2000 pages. See: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.kentstateuniversitypress.com/2011/hemingways-the-garden-of-eden/">http://www.kentstateuniversitypress.c...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="10976397849902720796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_78955308" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/78955308" />
    <a title="Daniel Villines" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines"><img alt="Daniel Villines" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536179787p2/2266055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/78955308?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Villines" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Villines" href="/user/show/2266055-daniel-villines">Daniel Villines</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/40057189-the-garden-of-eden">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78955308" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14086691660506123413">Is it fair to love a book because I know so much about the author? This is a story about a writer, David Bourne, who is the typical Hemingway hero: sensitive but self-sacrificing, creative, satisfied by simple pleasures, and stoic. David is honeymooning in the French Riviera at a secluded hotel outside of Cannes and spends his mornings writing.<br/><br/>If this were all the book had to offer, the story would still be good, but knowing about Hemingway’s life makes this book extraordinary. By having read ab</span>
  <span id="freeText14086691660506123413" style="display:none">Is it fair to love a book because I know so much about the author? This is a story about a writer, David Bourne, who is the typical Hemingway hero: sensitive but self-sacrificing, creative, satisfied by simple pleasures, and stoic. David is honeymooning in the French Riviera at a secluded hotel outside of Cannes and spends his mornings writing.<br /><br />If this were all the book had to offer, the story would still be good, but knowing about Hemingway’s life makes this book extraordinary. By having read about Hemingway’s life and his personality I had a perspective that permeated the story. I saw the critical parts of David Bourne as Hemingway, which served to humanize the story. There are also several elements of the plot that reflect back to events in Hemingway’s life.<br /><br />Maybe I’ve been played or conned into a feeling a certain way about this novel against my better judgement. It’s well documented that Hemingway was a master of publicity and many of his exploits are indeed captured by his biographers. Hemingway strove to remove the vacuum that usually seems to exists between a writer and his stories. But still, writing is art and the quality and beauty of art is absolutely dependent on its subject. Therefore, if Hemingway chose to use his life to humanize his art, then that is fair game. His stories represent a kind of self-portraiture.<br /><br />Thus, I loved this story as an episode in the life of David Bourne as well as a mirror of Hemingway’s own life. I loved the parts of the plot that are still edgy today and still challenge social norms. And I loved the parts of the plot that chronicle Hemingway’s writing process. I loved the setting in the French Riviera, and I loved the depiction of the hotel where simple hospitality, simple food, and simple drink created a kind of luxury and escape that money could never buy.<br /><br />Lastly, as an introduction to Hemingway, <i>The Garden of Eden</i> offers an approachable story. As readers, I think we can all relate to the art of writing as well as to the simple release of obligation that a good vacation can bring. Both of these themes exist in this book. Additionally, the ending is somewhat unique for a Hemingway novel. Yes, there will be some readers that will understandably find his style annoying, but others may find that Hemingway’s ability to gently nudge their imagination into alignment with his own intentions to be a different, more enjoyable form of writing altogether.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="14086691660506123413" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_19099620" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/19099620" />
    <a title="Karl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/156940-karl"><img alt="Karl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442478903p2/156940.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/19099620?book_show_action=true">Mar 31, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Karl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Karl" href="/user/show/156940-karl">Karl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer19099620" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer293808506911424131">Not my favorite Hemingway, though I understand why it was recommended to me: there is a lot of drinking in it. I think my problem with the book was that it kind of wanders around not really getting to the point, which is probably a result of the book being released posthumously. The book revolves around a newly wed couple vacationing in France/Spain in the late 1920's. The couple gets up every morning, goes for a swim, wanders the countryside, and drinks in the cafes. I did really enjoy the way </span>
  <span id="freeText293808506911424131" style="display:none">Not my favorite Hemingway, though I understand why it was recommended to me: there is a lot of drinking in it. I think my problem with the book was that it kind of wanders around not really getting to the point, which is probably a result of the book being released posthumously. The book revolves around a newly wed couple vacationing in France/Spain in the late 1920's. The couple gets up every morning, goes for a swim, wanders the countryside, and drinks in the cafes. I did really enjoy the way Hemingway creates a sense of atmosphere. However, Catherine (the main female character) is pretty much revealed to be out of her tiny little mind within the first 30 pages. David (the main male character) is a writer (and stand in for Hemingway) and I never really understood why he puts up with Catherine, let alone marries her. She's insane... from the get go, and not in a cute way. In a psycho way. He lets her run wild as he writes his novels. The book builds and builds up the pressure of jealousy until the eventual tragic end, which as a reader I saw coming and wondered why the characters didn't. I was loosing interest because of this, but the end of the book was definitely worth it. I also enjoyed Hemingway's writing on the nature of an artist creating, but was frustrated by the David/Catherine relationship. I could not understand why these two people were even together. </span>
  <a data-text-id="293808506911424131" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13614926-caidyn-he-him-his?shelf=queer-reads">queer-reads</a>,

<span id="freeTextContainer7535837751743333166">I love the queerness of it, but I don't like Hemingway's writing. It's annoying and soooo male. In a bad way. Basically, this is a queer story told by a cishet male. A product of the time, one that I wish I liked on my reread.<br/><br/><b>Original review:</b><br/><br/>I don't know how to describe this book honestly. My first Hemingway and I'm not sure exactly how to express how I feel about this book.<br/><br/>Simply written is the first thing that comes to mind. But, yet, it was well done and not too simple. There were complexitie</span>
  <span id="freeText7535837751743333166" style="display:none">I love the queerness of it, but I don't like Hemingway's writing. It's annoying and soooo male. In a bad way. Basically, this is a queer story told by a cishet male. A product of the time, one that I wish I liked on my reread.<br /><br /><b>Original review:</b><br /><br />I don't know how to describe this book honestly. My first Hemingway and I'm not sure exactly how to express how I feel about this book.<br /><br />Simply written is the first thing that comes to mind. But, yet, it was well done and not too simple. There were complexities hidden underneath the sentences that I knew were there but wasn't sure how to interpret them. The characters come next. Each character was alive on the page. I could connect with all of them. David brought out the writer in me, Catherine the feelings of being in the wrong body, and Marita being almost unsure how she fit in between the husband and wife.<br /><br />I wish this book had been completed before his death because now I want to know exactly how it ends. Does Catherine finally become the person she wants to be? Will David rewrite his work she burned and show that he will not be controlled by her? And Marita, will she stick around the couple that is slowly coming undone as she comes more into the scene?<br /><br />All I can say, is that I enjoyed this book greatly. It was a book I doubt I'll come across again.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_585407134" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/585407134" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7228708-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348397694p2/7228708.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/585407134?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/7228708-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer585407134" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10662823154026200622">After the publication of F. Scott Fitzgerald's <i> Tender Is The Night</i>, Hemingway wrote to Fitzgerald, a letter criticizing him on his failure as a writer. Here are a few select excerpts from that letter: <br/><i><br/>"Goddamn it you took liberties with people's pasts and futures that produced not people but damned marvellously faked case histories." <br/><br/>"...I've always claimed that you can't think."<br/><br/>"Invention is the finest thing but you cant invent something that would not actually happen."<br/><br/>"Of all people on earth </i></span>
  <span id="freeText10662823154026200622" style="display:none">After the publication of F. Scott Fitzgerald's <i> Tender Is The Night</i>, Hemingway wrote to Fitzgerald, a letter criticizing him on his failure as a writer. Here are a few select excerpts from that letter: <br /><i><br />"Goddamn it you took liberties with people's pasts and futures that produced not people but damned marvellously faked case histories." <br /><br />"...I've always claimed that you can't think."<br /><br />"Invention is the finest thing but you cant invent something that would not actually happen."<br /><br />"Of all people on earth you needed discipline in your work and instead you marry someone who is jealous of your work, wants to compete with you and ruins you."<br /></i><br />He went on to add: <i>"You know I never really thought so much of Gatsby at the time"</i><br /><br />One reason why this is important to note is that Hemingway saw his own work as a way of "beating" other writers in their trade. He set out to out-do and out-write his contemporaries, somewhat viewing writing as a competition. His critique of Fitzgerald for basing his main characters on mutual acquaintances Sara and Gerald Murphy comes with beautiful irony because, after writing that very letter, Hemingway went on to create his own semi-autobiographical oeuvre—one in which characters are, most obviously, based on his first and second marriages to Hadley Richardson and Pauline Pfieffer—in the 240,000 word manuscript of his unpublished <i>The Garden of Eden</i>.<br /><br />Published posthumously, the manuscript was finally released to the public in 1986 in a chopped up and incredibly condensed 70,000 words—a mere 30% of what he wrote for his manuscript—which his estate has been reluctant to release to the public, perhaps due to the controversial subject matter of characters swapping genders in their most intimate moments (Yep, Hemingway wrote a story about a guy taking it up the butt).<br /><br />In typical Hemingway fashion, his story features a protagonist who is a writer; a recently married David Bourne and wife Catherine honeymoon through a variety of beautiful European cities through Spain and France when they meet a tall dark and beautiful stranger, Marita, with whom they begin a tumultuous three-way love affair, while David spends his mornings writing; afternoons swimming; nights deciding with which one of the two women to sleep with—later on they decide to schedule their nights so as not to have jealousy arise.<br /><br />One thing I've come to learn about Hemingway is that he was a pioneer for postmodern metanarratives. <i>The Garden of Eden</i>, the book one is reading, is being written by David Bourne while the events are happening. At one point, he is questioned by Catherine about his progress, to which he replies: "I'm nearly up to now"—which caused my mind to explode due to the break of spatiotemporal narrative. The book we are reading is the book he is writing in the book we are reading. Wrap your mind around that and then try to tell me that Hemingway was not a postmodern writer.<br /><br />The book is also interwoven with another narrative David is writing, about a hunting trip to Africa he took with his father when he was young. If <i>The Garden of Eden</i> was Hemingway's attempt to out-write Fitzgerald and his <i>Tender Is The Night</i>, the Africa narrative about hunting down a majestically large elephant was Hemingway's attempt to out-write Faulkner (another rival) and his short story "The Bear"—which just like the Africa narrative, was a coming-of-age short about a young boy trying to hunt a majestically large animal (yes, it was a bear).<br /><br />In all honestly, I'd take Hemingway over Faulkner any day.<br /><br />TGoE is much different than Hemingway's usual dry prose—David at one point even makes a joke about not liking to use "wet words," an obvious reference to Hemingway. We have here a complex love story that goes deeply into gender issues of the era. Catherine has made it clear to David that she wants to become a full-time man, both in and out of the bedroom; she cuts her hair like a man, dyes it blonder and blonder until it becomes white, she tans herself until she becomes dark and wants to become darker. She convinces David to do the same, she wants them to become twins.<br /><br />As I mentioned before, the book we have is just about 30% of what was written. There was an entire parallel plot that was removed that dealt with another couple who—opposite of David and Catherine—decided to grow their hair long and both become women and included another third wheel for them to explore with.<br /><br />It would have been interesting to read how Hemingway would have handled a six-person partner swap, however, for now, the current version of TGoE would have to do.<br /><br />It's worth the read. I promise there is only one passage about fishing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10662823154026200622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1067315489" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1067315489" />
    <a title="Chrissie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie"><img alt="Chrissie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321799585p2/632247.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1067315489?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chrissie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chrissie" href="/user/show/632247-chrissie">Chrissie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=relationships">relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=2014-read">2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=spain">spain</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=lgtb">lgtb</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=downpour">downpour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/632247-chrissie?shelf=disliked">disliked</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1067315489" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10775031046231353191">There are numerous book descriptions here at GR. This says what you need to know: <br/><br/>"A sensational bestseller when it appeared in 1986, The Garden of Eden is the last uncompleted novel of Ernest Hemingway, which he worked on intermittently from 1946 until his death in 1961. Set on the Côte d'Azur in the 1920s, it is the story of a young American writer, David Bourne, his glamorous wife, Catherine, and the dangerous, erotic game they play when they fall in love with the same woman...."<br/><br/>The book was </span>
  <span id="freeText10775031046231353191" style="display:none">There are numerous book descriptions here at GR. This says what you need to know: <br /><br />"A sensational bestseller when it appeared in 1986, The Garden of Eden is the last uncompleted novel of Ernest Hemingway, which he worked on intermittently from 1946 until his death in 1961. Set on the Côte d'Azur in the 1920s, it is the story of a young American writer, David Bourne, his glamorous wife, Catherine, and the dangerous, erotic game they play when they fall in love with the same woman...."<br /><br />The book was uncompleted and was published posthumously. This is important to note. It does not read as a finished novel, even if it does contain some great lines. It is repetitive. The different threads are not drawn up properly. At the end, the message delivered is confused. It needs to be tightened up. Hemingway usually delivers a strong clear novel without numerous sidetracks, but not here.<br /><br />There is subdued eroticism which is tantalizing in sections, but then this gets sidetracked into the power struggle in a couple's relationship, and on a higher level between men and women in general. <br /><br />The narration by Patrick Wilson is perfectly acceptable.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10775031046231353191" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1067315489">

<div id="review_714830973" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/714830973" />
    <a title="Marcus Schantz" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12762682-marcus-schantz"><img alt="Marcus Schantz" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485023727p2/12762682.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/714830973?book_show_action=true">Sep 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marcus Schantz" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marcus Schantz" href="/user/show/12762682-marcus-schantz">Marcus Schantz</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer714830973" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17337344029323719151">I don't give many novels five star ratings but here it is deserved. This is a very heavy dose and caused an emotional reaction in myself that few books have. I was stunned while reading because of the issues and topics it deals with. Though written a long time ago, it's somehow modern and relevant. I know this story is loosely based on Hemingway's life and readers of a "A Moveable Feast" will quickly recognize and understand. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_45240335" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45240335" />
    <a title="Jessie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1368070-jessie"><img alt="Jessie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325644146p2/1368070.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/45240335?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jessie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jessie" href="/user/show/1368070-jessie">Jessie</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer45240335" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9326807121752280539">I could read this over and over and never get tired of it. It has been at the top of my favorite books list for a very long time. It's simple andIt's sparse and yet it speaks volumes about love and sex and men and women and our humanity and our imperfection. It's posthumous and even though it's different from everything else he wrote, it's still Papa.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_45240335'><a id="like_count_review_45240335" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/45240335?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">8 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F10775.The_Garden_of_Eden">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2289029449" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2289029449" />
    <a title="Ivana Books Are Magic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/56173179-ivana-books-are-magic"><img alt="Ivana Books Are Magic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578249636p2/56173179.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2289029449?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ivana Books Are Magic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ivana Books Are Magic" href="/user/show/56173179-ivana-books-are-magic">Ivana Books Are Magic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2289029449" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10506274560943074719">I can’t recall whether I knew The Garden of Eden was unfinished prior to reading it, but I do remember that it didn’t feel unfinished. Before reading this novel, I always thought of Hemingway in a way a lot people probably do, enjoying his prose but not seeing beyond his macho persona. It took me a while to take a closer look into this fascinating author and to learn more about him. I can say with certainly that The Garden of Eden changed the way I thought about Hemingway as a writer. This book </span>
  <span id="freeText10506274560943074719" style="display:none">I can’t recall whether I knew The Garden of Eden was unfinished prior to reading it, but I do remember that it didn’t feel unfinished. Before reading this novel, I always thought of Hemingway in a way a lot people probably do, enjoying his prose but not seeing beyond his macho persona. It took me a while to take a closer look into this fascinating author and to learn more about him. I can say with certainly that The Garden of Eden changed the way I thought about Hemingway as a writer. This book motivated me to read more of Hemingway when I thought I had read quite enough, but I will return to that later.<br /><br /><br />This novel follows a glamourous lifestyle of a writer David and his wife Catherine. Young and beautiful, they like to drink and have fun, all while spending their time in what resembles a never ending vacation. David writes but his writing doesn’t seem to get in the way of their drunken lifestyle, possibly because David has no financial need to write. The rich wife seems to be the one who pulls the strings, she makes most of decisions, while David seems to go along with whatever she wants. I immediately thought of Tender Is the Night, as these two individuals do strike a resemblance to the rich couple described in Fitzgerald’s Tender Is the Night. The only capital difference is perhaps in that Hemingway’s newlyweds are childless, while Fitzgerald’s couple is married with children. Naturally, there are many other subtle differences, but examining these two and the basic plot, one can’t help noticing the similarities. <br /><br /><br />It was only later that I learned that the similarities weren’t accidental. It is a well-known fact that these two writers were friends, and there was certainly some competition between Fitzgerald and Hemingway. Supposedly, Hemingway criticized Tender Is the Night, so perhaps The Garden of Eden can be seen as an attempt to write his own version of that novel and best Fitzgerald? The setting is similar, it is all about that Mediterranean sun. There is plenty of alcohol and everything smells of rich man’s escapism. Nevertheless, one cannot say that Hemingway copied Fitzgerald characters, for aren’t the husband and wife in Tender is The Night famously based on Frances and Zelda themselves? Moreover, it is quite possible that Hemingway drew from his own experiences, adding autobiographical elements to the novel. David from the book is clearly an alcoholic. The way he swamps women sure enough smells of Hemingway, but I’m jumping way ahead of myself here. <br /><br /><br /><br />I need to get to what impressed me the most about this novel and that is the examination of the gender identity. It might come to surprise to those who think of Hemingway as this macho guy, but The Garden of Eden examines homosexuality and gender identity. You see, the protagonist’s wife Catherine likes to pretend she is a guy, even in bed. Understandingly, this makes David uncomfortable. I had a feeling that David didn’t terribly mind the fact that Catherine enjoyed playing the tomboy (perhaps he even liked it), it was more their sexual life that David wasn’t comfortable with. You know, I’m uncertain as to what was behind his discomfort, whether it was because David liked ‘the reverse play’ but couldn’t bring himself to admit it or because he didn’t like it one bit. At any rate, David clearly wasn’t comfortable and Catherine doesn’t seem to care that much about how he feels. There is one scene where Catherine sodomizes him with a bottle. Yes, you’re read that right. <br /><br />However, David and Catherine’s relationship gets more complicated (as they it was not confused enough) when a third party is involved. They meet an heiress they both like. The heiress reminded me of the actress the protagonist of Tender Is the Night has an affair with, especially as the couple seemed equally enchanted with her at start. Anyhow, Catherine decides to sleep with the heiress (so that she can finally feel like a man?). Interestingly, it is not David who does it, starts an affair, it is Catherine. I will leave it at that, so I don’t spoil the whole plot. The more I read Hemingway, the more I realize that his portrayal of female characters is surprisingly complex. Both Catherine and the wife from Tender Is the Night seem to have a lot of issues (the men in both novels are no exception) but they are also fascinating characters, especially Catherine who comes off as such a strong-willed character. At times it seems like everyone’s Catherine’s puppet. Is it a case of Catherine knows what she wants and she knows how to get it? The more I read the novel the less sure was I of it. David is just as interesting as Catherine is. I mean he is quite different from other Hemingway’s protagonists, more vulnerable and perhaps also bitterer. I loved those parts where Hemingway (through David’s perspective) wrote about writing. That was wonderful. <br /><br /><br /><br /><br />Both David and Catherine left me puzzled, but perhaps I can say the same for the protagonists of Tender Is the Night. Since Fitzgerald and Hemingway were friends, it is interesting to wonder how they perceived each other’s writing. How autobiographical are these two novels? I still wonder about that. In one of his short stories, Hemingway writes how he had a friend who thought that the very rich lead different lives but that he was disappointed to find out they were the same as everyone. Was he referring to Fitzgerald? Money does talk in this novel. It is not the most important theme, but it’s there so you might want to pay attention to it. There were many parallels I could find between The Garden Of Eden and Tender Is the Night but I do see them as distinct works. When it comes to the writing style, these two writers couldn’t be more different one from another. Hemingway is all about simple but brilliant prose, Fitzgerald is all about poetical and eloquent prose. Still, the themes and the characters they write about are sometimes strikingly similar. <br /><br />This book was not what I expected at all, but I love it all the more for it. It made for a fascinating read, even if it was slightly disturbing at times. There is bitterness in it, hiding between the shadows of Mediterranean sun. Alcoholism is examined quite mercilessly and by the end of the novel there is plenty of sadness. I’m not sure I understood everything that the writer was trying to say, but I didn’t mind the ambiguity. It is quite possible that I’m reading too much into it, but The Garden of Eden seems like a profoundly complex and very ambitious work to me. This novel is different from most of the other Hemingway’s writing, yet not from all of it, as I’m beginning to discover. If anything, the uniqueness of The Garden Of Eden fascinates me, so I would say that it is different in a good way. Despite having a similar plot and setting as Tender Is the Night, this book feels absolutely original and fresh. I mean you don’t read much novels about a wife wanting to be a man in bed, do you? Let’s say I got a lot more from this book than I expected and I’m not complaining.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10506274560943074719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_537916686" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/537916686" />
    <a title="Shauna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4864955-shauna"><img alt="Shauna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1396375141p2/4864955.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/537916686?book_show_action=true">Feb 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shauna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shauna" href="/user/show/4864955-shauna">Shauna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer537916686" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12629984818989349571">This book is part reflection on the writing process, part portrait of a couple's descent into jealousy, and demise. Leading man David is successfully paring down his writing to its perfect, whole centre, while fracturing his already perfect marriage by adding another person to it.<br/><br/>Great lines: "He had not known just how greatly he had been divided and separated because once he started to work he wrote from an inner core which could not be split nor even marked nor scratched. He knew about this an</span>
  <span id="freeText12629984818989349571" style="display:none">This book is part reflection on the writing process, part portrait of a couple's descent into jealousy, and demise. Leading man David is successfully paring down his writing to its perfect, whole centre, while fracturing his already perfect marriage by adding another person to it.<br /><br />Great lines: "He had not known just how greatly he had been divided and separated because once he started to work he wrote from an inner core which could not be split nor even marked nor scratched. He knew about this and it was his strength since all the rest of him could be riven."<br /><br />David makes constant reference to his father, a man who "ran his life disastrously," and he seems to look up to him for that. At least his father wasn't so easily fractured. "His father was not vulnerable he knew and, unlike most people he had knew, only death could kill him."<br /><br />The Garden of Eden bears all the unmistakeable marks of Hemingway -- cool, tactile sense experiences ("...he carefully moved the cork between his thumb and first finger feeling the pinch of metal cap against his thumb and the long cold rounded promise of the bottle"), drinking, so much drinking, violent hunts played out in Africa, non-sentimental but warm sex scenes, lavish natural beauty. <br /><br />Amazing -- highly recommend to pretty well anybody.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12629984818989349571" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tRDf4+byRZXCAYdLdZILbHxe4KgQA0KpUqu8UG8q/cfXEcqfLUIGnXlgHooQUC2sytQKPYGKu3Imr4MQTX+tfQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fT2ZMR3aMLp8sbYTwvhg2bbFFeYuXNoDU/lKzNds5BofPIxN1mpzssfQL9KnOkYZAE//c7/VI9gn/XWM9Tm0oA==')})
  }

Unwilling to take no for an answer, propelled by a passion for his subject, and already deep into his research, Shields wrote again and this time, to his delight, the answer came back: &quot;O.K.&quot; For the next year—a year that ended up being Vonnegut\'s last—Shields had access to Vonnegut and his letters.And So It Goes is the culmination of five years of research and writing—the first-ever biography of the life of Kurt Vonnegut. Vonnegut resonates with readers of all generations from the baby boomers who grew up with him to high-school and college students who are discovering his work for the first time. Vonnegut\'s concise collection of personal essays, Man Without a Country, published in 2006, spent fifteen weeks on the New York Times bestseller list and has sold more than 300,000 copies to date. The twenty-first century has seen interest in and scholarship about Vonnegut\'s works grow even stronger, and this is the first book to examine in full the life of one of the most influential <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1090902.From_Caligari_to_Hitler"><img alt="From Caligari to Hitler: A Psychological History of the German Film" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1348289245l/1090902._SX98_.jpg" />

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_1090902'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1090902.From_Caligari_to_Hitler?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">From Caligari to Hitler: A Psychological History of the German Film<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/30725.Siegfried_Kracauer?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Siegfried Kracauer<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.89 avg rating &mdash; 675 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1947\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer6151817912746248729\">A landmark, now classic, study of the rich cinematic history of the Weimar Republic, From Caligari to Hitler was first published by Princeton University Press in 1947. Siegfried Kracauer--a prominent German film critic and member of Walter Benjamin\'s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText6151817912746248729\" style=\"display:none\">A landmark, now classic, study of the rich cinematic history of the Weimar Republic, From Caligari to Hitler was first published by Princeton University Press in 1947. Siegfried Kracauer--a prominent German film critic and member of Walter Benjamin\'s and Theodor Adorno\'s intellectual circle--broke new ground in exploring the connections between film aesthetics, the prevailing psychological state of Germans in the Weimar era, and the evolving social and political reality of the time. Kracauer\'s pioneering book, which examines German history from 1921 to 1933 in light of such movies as The Cabinet of Dr. Caligari, M, Metropolis, and The Blue Angel, has never gone out of print. Now, over half a century after its first appearance, this beautifully designed and entirely new edition reintroduces Kracauer for the twenty-first century. Film scholar Leonardo Quaresima places Kracauer in context in a critical introduction, and updates the book further with a new bibliography, index, and list of inaccuracies that crept into the first edition. This volume is a must-have for the film historian, film theorist, or cinema enthusiast.\n\nIn From Caligari to Hitler, Siegfried Kracauer made a startling (and still controversial) claim: films as a popular art provide insight into the unconscious motivations and fantasies of a nation. In films of the 1920s, he traced recurring visual and narrative tropes that expressed, he argued, a fear of chaos and a desire for order, even at the price of authoritarian rule. The book has become an undisputed classic of film historiography, laying the foundations for the serious study of film.\n\nKracauer was an important film critic in Weimar Germany. A Jew, he escaped the rise of Nazism, fleeing to Paris in 1933. Later, in anguish after Benjamin\'s suicide, he made his way to New York, where he remained until his death in 1966. He wrote From Caligari to Hitler while working as a &quot;special assistant&quot; to the curator of the Museum of Modern Art\'s film division. He was also on the editorial board of Bollingen Series. Despite many critiques of its attempt to link movies to historical outcomes, From Caligari to Hitler remains Kracauer\'s best-known and most influential book, and a seminal work in the study of film. Princeton published a revised edition of his Theory of Film: The Redemption of Physical Reality in 1997.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"6151817912746248729\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));;       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/1455.Ernest_Hemingway">About Ernest Hemingway</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/1455.Ernest_Hemingway"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1574217836p3/1455.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17436628156121199620">Ernest Miller Hemingway was an American author and journalist. His economical and understated style had a strong influence on 20th-century fiction, while his life of adventure and his public image influenced later generations. Hemingway produced most of his work between the mid-1920s and the mid-1950s, and won the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1954. He published seven novels, six short story collec</span>
  <span id="freeText17436628156121199620" style="display:none">Ernest Miller Hemingway was an American author and journalist. His economical and understated style had a strong influence on 20th-century fiction, while his life of adventure and his public image influenced later generations. Hemingway produced most of his work between the mid-1920s and the mid-1950s, and won the Nobel Prize in Literature in 1954. He published seven novels, six short story collections and two non-fiction works. Three novels, four collections of short stories and three non-fiction works were published posthumously. Many of these are considered classics of American literature.<br /><br />Hemingway was raised in Oak Park, Illinois, and after high school he reported for a few months for The Kansas City Star, before leaving for the Italian front to enlist with the World War I ambulance drivers. In 1918, he was seriously wounded and returned home. His wartime experiences formed the basis for his novel A Farewell to Arms. In 1922, he married Hadley Richardson, the first of his four wives. The couple moved to Paris, where he worked as a foreign correspondent, and fell under the influence of the modernist writers and artists of the 1920s "Lost Generation" expatriate community. The Sun Also Rises, Hemingway's first novel, was published in 1926.<br /><br />After his 1927 divorce from Hadley Richardson, Hemingway married Pauline Pfeiffer. They divorced after he returned from Spanish Civil War where he had acted as a journalist, and after which he wrote For Whom the Bell Tolls. Martha Gellhorn became his third wife in 1940. They separated when he met Mary Welsh in London during World War II; during which he was present at the Normandy Landings and liberation of Paris.<br /><br />Shortly after the publication of The Old Man and the Sea in 1952, Hemingway went on safari to Africa, where he was almost killed in two plane crashes that left him in pain and ill-health for much of the rest of his life. Hemingway had permanent residences in Key West, Florida, and Cuba during the 1930s and 1940s, but in 1959 he moved from Cuba to Ketchum, Idaho, where he committed suicide in the summer of 1961.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17436628156121199620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
